  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_7cd3777c-43ba-4ff0-b3ec-92b812fca231'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2406376205897802466">The Collected Stories of Amy Hempel gathers together the complete work of a writer whose voice is as singular and astonishing as any in American fiction. Hempel, fiercely admired by writers and reviewers, has a sterling reputation that is based on four very short collections of stories, roughly fifteen thousand stunning sentences, written over a period of nearly three deca</span>
  <span id="freeText2406376205897802466" style="display:none">The Collected Stories of Amy Hempel gathers together the complete work of a writer whose voice is as singular and astonishing as any in American fiction. Hempel, fiercely admired by writers and reviewers, has a sterling reputation that is based on four very short collections of stories, roughly fifteen thousand stunning sentences, written over a period of nearly three decades. These are stories about people who make choices that seem inevitable, whose longings and misgivings evoke eternal human experience. With compassion, wit, and the acutest eye, Hempel observes the marriages, minor disasters, and moments of revelation in an uneasy America. When "Reasons to Live, " Hempel's first collection, was published in 1985, readers encountered a pitch-perfect voice in fiction and an unsettling assessment of the culture. That collection includes "San Francisco," which Alan Cheuse in "The Chicago Tribune" called "arguably the finest short story composed by any living writer." In "At the Gates of the Animal Kingdom, " her second collection, frequently compared to the work of Raymond Carver, Hempel refined and developed her unique grace and style and her unerring instinct for the moment that defines a character. Also included here, in their entirety, are the collections "Tumble Home" and "The Dog of the Marriage." As Rick Moody says of the title novella in Tumble Home, "the leap in mastery, in seriousness, and sheer literary purpose was inspiring to behold.... And yet," he continues, ""The Dog of the Marriage, " the fourth collection, is even better than the other three...a triumph, in fact." "The Collected Stories of Amy Hempel" is the perfect opportunity for readers of contemporary American fiction to catch up to one of its masters. Moody's passionate and illuminating introduction celebrates both the appeal and the importance of Hempel's work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2406376205897802466" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18652614" />
    <a title="Adrianne Mathiowetz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30900-adrianne-mathiowetz"><img alt="Adrianne Mathiowetz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442287826p2/30900.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18652614?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adrianne Mathiowetz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adrianne Mathiowetz" href="/user/show/30900-adrianne-mathiowetz">Adrianne Mathiowetz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743291637" href="/book/show/841644.The_Collected_Stories_of_Amy_Hempel">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Hemingway fans, people who hate Hemingway</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Adrianne by: </span>
      Rick Moody, who for the record writes an annoying introduction.
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18652614" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9921410168067238733">This is one of those books that has you rereading sentences over and over again, not because you couldn't parse their basic meaning, but because you suspect that a second reading will glean another, more subtle bit of information. It will also make you want to own a dog. It will also have you falling in love with Amy Hempel and wanting to make her your bride, in a house on the countryside with a weedy garden and a swamp nearby.<br/><br/>I started reading <i>God of Small Things</i> within five minutes of finishin</span>
  <span id="freeText9921410168067238733" style="display:none">This is one of those books that has you rereading sentences over and over again, not because you couldn't parse their basic meaning, but because you suspect that a second reading will glean another, more subtle bit of information. It will also make you want to own a dog. It will also have you falling in love with Amy Hempel and wanting to make her your bride, in a house on the countryside with a weedy garden and a swamp nearby.<br /><br />I started reading <i>God of Small Things</i> within five minutes of finishing Hempel's collection of short stories, and as soon as I finish <i>God of Small Things</i> I intend to reread Hempel: as much as I love writing flowery, adjective-ridden prose, I'd rather return to Hempel's universe of somehow showing without showing, and saying without saying. <br /><br />Hempel's stories will make you want to write differently: what higher praise can I give?</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_921769897" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/921769897" />
    <a title="Melanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4093022-melanie"><img alt="Melanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566338030p2/4093022.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/921769897?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanie" href="/user/show/4093022-melanie">Melanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743291637" href="/book/show/841644.The_Collected_Stories_of_Amy_Hempel">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer921769897" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9779227682565917218">Amy Hempel writes intriguing, beautifully constructed sentences. Piecemeal they are pretty darn awesome. Here's the rub. I'm not sure if how one incredible sentence leads into another is coherent enough for me. I found a major disconnect. It's like being walked into a conversational corner, like recently when my sister in law compared the intrigues of Tupperware parties with Australian parliament. There may be true enough correlations, but I just couldn't get there. Either place.<br/><br/>On the other han</span>
  <span id="freeText9779227682565917218" style="display:none">Amy Hempel writes intriguing, beautifully constructed sentences. Piecemeal they are pretty darn awesome. Here's the rub. I'm not sure if how one incredible sentence leads into another is coherent enough for me. I found a major disconnect. It's like being walked into a conversational corner, like recently when my sister in law compared the intrigues of Tupperware parties with Australian parliament. There may be true enough correlations, but I just couldn't get there. Either place.<br /><br />On the other hand, the author is provocative, secretive, suggestive, there is an open invitation to interpret the narrative, to end the started sentence, to leap into supposition - there is charm in that, but one after the other (this hefty a collection), that's a lot of coloured plastic and politics. <br /><br />More than once I wanted to put this aside but I've read nothing but glowing reviews, so it really does come down to plain preference, preferring blood sports to shadow boxing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9779227682565917218" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_196072199" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/196072199" />
    <a title="Charlotte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5986769-charlotte"><img alt="Charlotte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1358365677p2/5986769.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/196072199?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlotte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlotte" href="/user/show/5986769-charlotte">Charlotte</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743291637" href="/book/show/841644.The_Collected_Stories_of_Amy_Hempel">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer196072199" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9063042735399001042">Am I the only reader who doesn't take pleasure in reading Amy Hempel? She is always praised for writing "the perfect sentence," for the way she distills a story to its poetic essence, for writing precise little gems. (Some stories are less than two pages long.) How can I describe my aversion? Is it that I feel like I'm being toyed with? That Hempel's spareness is a literary exercise? There's a chill in her writing that comes from that spareness, I think. There was one story that was superb, "In </span>
  <span id="freeText9063042735399001042" style="display:none">Am I the only reader who doesn't take pleasure in reading Amy Hempel? She is always praised for writing "the perfect sentence," for the way she distills a story to its poetic essence, for writing precise little gems. (Some stories are less than two pages long.) How can I describe my aversion? Is it that I feel like I'm being toyed with? That Hempel's spareness is a literary exercise? There's a chill in her writing that comes from that spareness, I think. There was one story that was superb, "In the Cemetery Where Al Jolson is Buried," in which she makes her one and only visit to her best friend, who is dying alone in a hospital. It's unflinching and autobiographical. But again, I felt her at a great remove. She's the master of "show it, don't tell it." I experienced the narrator's jubilation at running away from death, but the specter of guilt was only inferred. It's a powerful trick. But I end up feeling I've had no communion with this writer, only that I've been acted upon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9063042735399001042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3542925326" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3542925326" />
    <a title="Kiran Bhat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/81560301-kiran-bhat"><img alt="Kiran Bhat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525335374p2/81560301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3542925326?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kiran Bhat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kiran Bhat" href="/user/show/81560301-kiran-bhat">Kiran Bhat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3542925326" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11354535833028703643">Amy Hempel is a master of the sentence, as well as under-statement. She can create paragraphs that pack more emotional sentiment than entire novels. Part of this is her ability to render the verisimilitude of a moment and place it directly into her character's eyes. She can also be funny and sarcastic too. There are few contemporary writers who have as much versatility as she does, at least in the small slice of Americana she chooses to write from.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_28523258" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28523258" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1033675-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325271531p2/1033675.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28523258?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/1033675-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">aspiring writers</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28523258" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10131263215189928158">The reason for reading this book was because I could not otherwise get <i>away</i> from this lady until doing so. One such instance involved an innocent perusal of Raymond Carver's wikipedia entry, and there was Hempel and Gordon Lish sitting one booth over and trying to look conspicuously casual. Another time I was cruising Palahniuk.com (feeling all manly and disenfranchised, of course), and there she was again, rocking back and forth on her heels expectantly after blurting out an awkward "Hi!". This</span>
  <span id="freeText10131263215189928158" style="display:none">The reason for reading this book was because I could not otherwise get <i>away</i> from this lady until doing so. One such instance involved an innocent perusal of Raymond Carver's wikipedia entry, and there was Hempel and Gordon Lish sitting one booth over and trying to look conspicuously casual. Another time I was cruising Palahniuk.com (feeling all manly and disenfranchised, of course), and there she was again, rocking back and forth on her heels expectantly after blurting out an awkward "Hi!". This is obviously all in jest, as I'm not yet unbalanced enough to truly believe that writers stalk potential readers (well...except for maybe a couple of those Paranormal Romance folks...). The motivation to read this book gave rise to questions in my mind about how readers are led to explore a previously unfamiliar writer based solely on a lumped association with writers that the reader already knows. Perhaps I just need an additional hobby.<br /><br />Seriously, this book is a work of great beauty. This collection unites Hempel's previous collections into one volume which spans the years of 1985-2005. She is considered to be one of those writers that slaves over every word and sentence, and this results in paragraphs that explode with meaning. These are basically stories about people trying to reconcile the circumstances of their lives with a demeanor of quiet intensity. Seemingly mundane tasks that are undertaken by characters within these stories, such as knitting or gardening, reflect and amplify the overall theme of each story. Some of these themes include death, the decay of marriages and relationships, and the bond between humans and pets. Despite this description, Hempel is not just a one-trick "gloom and doom" pony. There are light-hearted moments within this book that are equally touching as well. An example of this would be the first line of 'Tonight Is a Favor to Holly' and possibly one of my favorite sentences in this book:<br /><br />"A blind date is coming to pick me up, and unless my hair grows an inch by seven o'clock, I am not going to answer the door."<br /><br />How great is that? Probably all of us know at least one person who has said a variation on these words at some point in life.<br /><br />I'm really conflicted about this rating, as this is easily a five star book by anyone's standards. My personal rating metric has always been that a five star book for me is one that has fundamentally changed the way that I view the world or myself upon completion. This book did not quite accomplish this, although I found it very emotionally moving. This is actually saying something, as I'm not usually an "emotionally moving literature" kind of guy.<br /><br />The only possible complaint that I could have here is that most of these stories are written in a first-person perspective from the point of view of a woman that seems to be roughly the same age as Hempel would have been when these stories were written. This caused some conflict on my part as I began to conflate the author with the subject matter and would have to remind myself that these are stories, not necessarily the private journals of an incredibly gifted writer. The whole first-person confessional thing always makes me a little uncomfortable, as I think back to the young fellow whose early, false starts at fiction always involved an angst-y male protagonist in his early twenties. This is probably my problem as a reader, rather than Hempel's problem. <br /><br />David, I've put a lot of thought into the one thing that I could say to renew your interest in this book. It should be somewhat lurid as you are a male(I knew that marketing class would come in handy someday), yet at the same time not a total spoiler. So here goes: You should continue reading this book because when you reach the final story, 'Offertory', you will surely say the same thing that I did - "Twelve acts of sexual congress in the same night!?! Good Lord! Has that guy never heard of a refractory period or what?".</span>
  <a data-text-id="10131263215189928158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6699954?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian Mullet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian Mullet" href="/user/show/176306-ian-mullet">Ian Mullet</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6699954" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4406318245976532935">i kind of just want to copy one of her stories and so that's what i'm going to do. her stories are pretty short and this may be the shortest of them all. it's called:<br/><br/>"The Man in Bogata"<br/><br/>The police and emergency service people fail to make a dent. The voice of the pleading spouse does not have the hoped-for effect. The woman remains on the ledge -- though not, she threatens, for long.<br/><br/>I imagine that I am the one who must talk the woman down. I see it, and it happens like this.<br/><br/>I tell the woman about</span>
  <span id="freeText4406318245976532935" style="display:none">i kind of just want to copy one of her stories and so that's what i'm going to do. her stories are pretty short and this may be the shortest of them all. it's called:<br /><br />"The Man in Bogata"<br /><br />The police and emergency service people fail to make a dent. The voice of the pleading spouse does not have the hoped-for effect. The woman remains on the ledge -- though not, she threatens, for long.<br /><br />I imagine that I am the one who must talk the woman down. I see it, and it happens like this.<br /><br />I tell the woman about a man in Bogota. He was a wealthy man, an industrialist who was kidnapped and held for ransom. It was not a TV drama; his wife could not call the bank and, in twenty-four hours, have one million dollars. It took months. The man had a heart condition, and the kipnappers had to keep the man alive.<br /><br />Listen to this, I tell the woman on the ledge. His captors made him quit smoking. They changed his diet and made him exercise every day. They held him that way for three months.<br /><br />When the ransom was paid and the man was released, his doctor looked him over. He found the man to be in excellent health. I tell the woman what the doctor said then -- the kidnap was the best thing to happen to that man.<br /><br />[then three little stars follow along with three more sentences which i omit for copyright reasons.]<br /><br />Her writing is the best I've come across in a while -- "in my head there's a balcony I fall off of when I speak."<br /><br />Not only is she telling a story but she's also feeling it with you, for example, from "The Most Girl Part of You": "Since his mother died I have seen him steam a cucumber thinking it was zucchini. That's the kind of thing that turns my heart right over."<br /><br />Maybe she's a sentimentalist, maybe she's beautiful, but when i read her i find myself wondering, how would one know the difference?<br /><br />She's also gotten my thinking a lot about irony and I wonder, really, doubt, whether it exists at all. Maybe the real trick to irony is to not be ironic at all, just to believe something crazy or to believe it in a way that everyone thinks, "oh, that must be ironic." Let the reader do your ironizing for you...<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4406318245976532935" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743291637" href="/book/show/841644.The_Collected_Stories_of_Amy_Hempel">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/166376-david?shelf=read-in-2007">read-in-2007</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/166376-david?shelf=anthologies-and-collections">anthologies-and-collections</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6591209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14840594269945106533">I know it's a cliche, but some of these stories just took my breath away. "In the Cemetery where Al Jolson is buried" is just extraordinary, but there are at least a half a dozen other stories which are just as good. <br/><br/>This book contains all four collections of short stories written by Hempel over the last 20 years and has been praised to the skies. Deservedly so, IMO. Some of the stories are less than a page long, but they all pack a punch.<br/><br/>A couple of months later, and I'm downgrading this review</span>
  <span id="freeText14840594269945106533" style="display:none">I know it's a cliche, but some of these stories just took my breath away. "In the Cemetery where Al Jolson is buried" is just extraordinary, but there are at least a half a dozen other stories which are just as good. <br /><br />This book contains all four collections of short stories written by Hempel over the last 20 years and has been praised to the skies. Deservedly so, IMO. Some of the stories are less than a page long, but they all pack a punch.<br /><br />A couple of months later, and I'm downgrading this review to 4 stars. Why?<br /><br />Too many dogs.<br />More seriously, several of these stories were unnecessarily opaque, almost to a Faulknerian degree. If one goes to the trouble of reading through a story three times, then one shouldn't still be in the dark about who did what to whom and when. I realize that this kind of opacity may sometimes be deliberate, in the service of the story, but it's bothersome enough to make me revise my rating downwards. There are still some excellent stories in this book, but there are some duds as well. So no 5 stars, in the interests of reserving some kind of meaning for that rating.<br /><br />(There were a lot of dogs, though only a frivolous person would use that a basis for rating. Right? :))</span>

<div id="review_881963177" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/881963177" />
    <a title="Corey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8386063-corey"><img alt="Corey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528466470p2/8386063.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/881963177?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Corey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Corey" href="/user/show/8386063-corey">Corey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8386063-corey?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8386063-corey?shelf=laughed-out-loud">laughed-out-loud</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer881963177" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8596396088116262001">I workshopped a story about a dog in one of my fiction writing classes at school last semester, and several people informed me that I needed to read Amy Hempel. They told me that she wrote these great stories that always had dogs in them. <br/><br/>They always have dogs in them? I asked. That's like her trademark?<br/><br/>And while I was busy sort of mocking this idea, one of my friends pointed out that I had written a story about a dog, and I found myself out of excuses.<br/><br/>And wow, am I ever glad that I read the wor</span>
  <span id="freeText8596396088116262001" style="display:none">I workshopped a story about a dog in one of my fiction writing classes at school last semester, and several people informed me that I needed to read Amy Hempel. They told me that she wrote these great stories that always had dogs in them. <br /><br />They always have dogs in them? I asked. That's like her trademark?<br /><br />And while I was busy sort of mocking this idea, one of my friends pointed out that I had written a story about a dog, and I found myself out of excuses.<br /><br />And wow, am I ever glad that I read the works of Amy Hempel. I'm never quite sure how to review short story collections, let alone a book that contains three and a half decades of a writer's work, but here's what I can say about this book: read it. The prose is stunning, each sentence contains multitudes. I want to read this entire book aloud to somebody who has never heard of her before. I want to smack my earlier self in the face for poking fun at her. The longer stories especially--namely, Tumble Home and Offertory--are masterpieces, something beyond "flawless." I think she did what she did better than Raymond Carver.<br /><br />I know one thing--I need to scrap that dog story I wrote and rethink everything.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8596396088116262001" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Chaikin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Chaikin" href="/user/show/18695911-daniel-chaikin">Daniel Chaikin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2406457736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4877600102851028696">"<i>My heart—I thought it stopped. So I got in my car and headed for God.</i>" So entered Amy Hempel into the book world, a master of the line to the point of hyper-intense concision. Hempel was one of the great short story writers of the 1980's whose career went beyond the popularity of form. When short stories weren't selling so well the in the 1990's, she tried to transform into a longer form writer, coming out with only the 80-page story <i>Tumble Home</i>, a good illustration of why that's not her streng</span>
  <span id="freeText4877600102851028696" style="display:none">"<i>My heart—I thought it stopped. So I got in my car and headed for God.</i>" So entered Amy Hempel into the book world, a master of the line to the point of hyper-intense concision. Hempel was one of the great short story writers of the 1980's whose career went beyond the popularity of form. When short stories weren't selling so well the in the 1990's, she tried to transform into a longer form writer, coming out with only the 80-page story <i>Tumble Home</i>, a good illustration of why that's not her strength. It's a good story in the end, but the getting their requires the reader to move through numerous hyper-intense short outbursts all going different directions. <br /><br />But in the short form she is a special voice, clever, fun, passive in complex and fascinating ways, and full of memorable lines, including wonderful opening lines (all hinted at in some of her titles). She can be sensual, but mostly she is scoffing and blessing at the same time all life's normal difficulties. Another gem that was languishing on the shelf way too long. <i>
  <blockquote>Mrs. Deane scans the written portion of my test. She says I skipped a question, the one that says, "Would you prefer to: (a) Think about your plans for tomorrow, (b) Think about what you would do if you had a million dollars, (c) Think about how it would feel to be held up at gunpoint?"<br /><br />I say, "I want the job for the person who picks (b)."</blockquote>
</i><br /><br />-----------------------------------------------<br /><br />34. <b>The Collected Stories of Amy Hempel</b><br /><b>Forward</b> Rick Moody<br /><b>published</b>: 2006<br /><b>format</b>: 412 page ARC Paperback<br /><b>acquired</b>: 2007 from a library book sale<br /><b>read</b>: May 29-Jun 14<br /><b>rating</b>: 4½ <br /><br />This is a collection of four books of stories:<br />- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33300.Reasons_to_Live" title="Reasons to Live by Amy Hempel" rel="nofollow noopener">Reasons to Live</a> (1985)<br />- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33303.At_the_Gates_of_the_Animal_Kingdom_Stories" title="At the Gates of the Animal Kingdom Stories by Amy Hempel" rel="nofollow noopener">At the Gates of the Animal Kingdom: Stories</a> (1990)<br />- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33302.Tumble_Home_A_Novella_and_Short_Stories" title="Tumble Home A Novella and Short Stories by Amy Hempel" rel="nofollow noopener">Tumble Home: A Novella and Short Stories</a> (1997)<br />- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33301.The_Dog_of_the_Marriage_Stories" title="The Dog of the Marriage Stories by Amy Hempel" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dog of the Marriage: Stories</a> (2005)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4877600102851028696" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1940209710" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1940209710" />
    <a title="Vincent Scarpa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5454934-vincent-scarpa"><img alt="Vincent Scarpa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586402748p2/5454934.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1940209710?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vincent Scarpa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vincent Scarpa" href="/user/show/5454934-vincent-scarpa">Vincent Scarpa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454934-vincent-scarpa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1940209710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16615631648321996390">It'd been a while since I reread the entirety of Hempel's <i>Collected</i>. There are certain stories I revisit with regularity—"In the Cemetery...," "Today Will Be a Quiet Day," "The Harvest," "The Most Girl Part of You," "The Dog of the Marriage"—but I felt an urge to go through them all again, and I'm so glad I did. Not that it delivered me unto anything I didn't already know—Amy Hempel is a genius, and on the Mount Rushmore of short story writers, DUH—but it was a pleasure to reread stories that ha</span>
  <span id="freeText16615631648321996390" style="display:none">It'd been a while since I reread the entirety of Hempel's <i>Collected</i>. There are certain stories I revisit with regularity—"In the Cemetery...," "Today Will Be a Quiet Day," "The Harvest," "The Most Girl Part of You," "The Dog of the Marriage"—but I felt an urge to go through them all again, and I'm so glad I did. Not that it delivered me unto anything I didn't already know—Amy Hempel is a genius, and on the Mount Rushmore of short story writers, DUH—but it was a pleasure to reread stories that hadn't grabbed me the first, second, third time I read them but, for whatever reason, absolutely did this time around. "Jesus Is Waiting" is one. What a story. In the same way that Amy has said she could not have written "Today Will Be A Quiet Day" had Mary Robison not written "Widower," "Jesus Is Waiting" feels like a take on Robison's <i>Why Did I Ever</i>:<br /><br /><blockquote>“In a tornado outside Baltimore, in a broken neighborhood off I-95, I asked the attendant in a Mobil station, ‘Where’s anywhere else?’ The man didn’t even point.”</blockquote><br /><br />Similarly, I don't remember "The Uninvited" as having had much of an effect on me in previous readings, but this time it stopped me in my tracks. How had I failed to notice how masterful a story it is? <br /><br />But the big gift—beyond an excuse to read for probably the tenth time the exquisite and flawless "Tumble Home"—was becoming reacquainted with "Offertory," a story that I've always admired but which always felt rather inscrutable to me. I can't claim to "understand" all of it this time around either, but my god, it's now in the ranks of my favorite Hempel stories. The way she writes about sex in the story—I'm sorry, but it makes even Gaitskill pale in comparison. "Offertory" also includes what is probably my favorite line in all of Hempel: “It is possible to imagine a person so entirely that the image resists attempts to dislodge it.” Ain't <i>that</i> the fucking truth.<br /><br />All of this to say that if you haven't yet read Amy Hempel, or if you've only read the few stories that are frequently anthologized, just get this book, give yourself over to it. <br /><br />It's a master class in writing—and in humanity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16615631648321996390" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Erik F." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7324260-erik-f"><img alt="Erik F." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346629842p2/7324260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer541984692" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12623743814725430735">There is some really terrific material in this collection; Hempel has a remarkable talent for refreshing turns of phrase and revealing details that lesser writers would never consider. At their best the stories exude sprightly spontaneity along with shrewd perceptions of human emotions and shortcomings. Hempel's voice is consistently wry, tough, and wise (but never self-important or excessively pessimistic). She's charming and disarming, yet unafraid of packing emotional punches; candid honesty </span>
  <span id="freeText12623743814725430735" style="display:none">There is some really terrific material in this collection; Hempel has a remarkable talent for refreshing turns of phrase and revealing details that lesser writers would never consider. At their best the stories exude sprightly spontaneity along with shrewd perceptions of human emotions and shortcomings. Hempel's voice is consistently wry, tough, and wise (but never self-important or excessively pessimistic). She's charming and disarming, yet unafraid of packing emotional punches; candid honesty and artful simplicity infuse these miniature works, which explore some of life's little joys and disappointments with bracing humor and sensitivity.<br /><br />Personal favorites: "In the Cemetery Where Al Jolson is Buried," "Offertory," "Rapture of the Deep," "Nashville Gone to Ashes," "The Harvest," "Jesus Is Waiting," "The Most Girl Part of You," "Reference #388475848-5," "Church Cancels Cow," "The Man in Bogotá"</span>
  <a data-text-id="12623743814725430735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2049762093" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7692851202050656839">Amy Hempel's powerful fiction will make you cry, will make you laugh, and finally will break your heart and then send it out to dry.<br/><br/>Readers, she will teach you what it means to read fiction again.<br/><br/>Writers, beware - there is a Ramadhir Singh inside her that wants to tell you ki "Rehne do beta, tumse na ho payega..." Or as Chuck Palahniuk, the writer who introduced me to Amy Hempel's fiction says, "You will write, but you will never write this well."</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer951498478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13548930848019809146">Contrarian Opinion #1: Reasons for Living is the weakest collection of the four. Still good, with the obvious classic "In the Cemetery Where Al Jolson Was Buried," but in moments it feels too sketchy, or too disjointed, or too oddly paced. <br/><br/>Contrarian Opinion #2: The Dog of the Marriage is, by a wide margin, the strongest collection of the four. This is the work of a writer who has learned to craft not just beautiful sentences, but powerful stories that focus on grief and recovery, major tragedy </span>
  <span id="freeText13548930848019809146" style="display:none">Contrarian Opinion #1: Reasons for Living is the weakest collection of the four. Still good, with the obvious classic "In the Cemetery Where Al Jolson Was Buried," but in moments it feels too sketchy, or too disjointed, or too oddly paced. <br /><br />Contrarian Opinion #2: The Dog of the Marriage is, by a wide margin, the strongest collection of the four. This is the work of a writer who has learned to craft not just beautiful sentences, but powerful stories that focus on grief and recovery, major tragedy and small triumph, dwelling on the past and looking towards the future. Along with Lydia Davis and Philip Roth, Amy Hempel is a terrific example of an author who only got better as her career went on.<br /><br />Not-so-contrarian opinion: Hempel's prose is a magnificent thing. She's up there with the greats as one of the all-time best prose stylists. I wasn't fully sold on her as a storyteller until I read the Dog of the Marriage, but her prose, man, holy shit. Her sentences are beautiful, each word weighed for maximum impact, stripped down to their barest essence while still maintaining the wild, ebullient spirit of great writing. No wonder authors from Chuck Palahniuk to Rick Moody have lined up to sing her praises: she deserves it.<br /><br />I'll give this more of a review as time goes on (hopefully), this is just what I was thinking for now. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13548930848019809146" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText13449133080159767539" style="display:none">This has all four of Amy Hempel's short story collections in one volume. I am assigning it four stars mostly on the strength of her first collection, <i>Reasons to Live</i> published way back in the 1980s. <br /><br />I kept thinking "a female Richard Brautigan" while reading her early stories. Quirky subjects and bizarre dialogue. You just never know where she's going to go next. Three of my four favorite stories in the whole book are from this first collection. The other three collections just don't have that edge, and I didn't enjoy them as much. However, as Rick Moody says in the intro, with Amy Hempel "it's all about the sentences." Sometimes if the story itself is not particularly great, she'll nail you with occasional sentences that will knock you out.<br /><br />My four favorites: <br /><br />1)Tonight is a Favor to Holly<br /><br />2)Beg, Sl Tog, Inc, Cont, Rep ("I planned to knit myself a mailbox and a car, perhaps even a dog and a lead to walk him.")<br /><br />3)Today Will Be a Quiet Day<br /><br />4)Sportsman (hilarious dialogue)<br /><br /></span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer515539586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10188564497526834532">Annie Dillard writes in one of her books about a young student approaching his famous writer/professor. "So you think I can be a great writer?" asks the student. "I don't know. Do you like sentences?"" answers his teacher. I thought about this as I read Amy Hempel's book because I paused after so many sentences while reading. Paused not only for "wow" but paused also for "mmm" and paused also for "ouch", like when the sentence evoked the memory of hurt (to you or by you). If you like to write, t</span>
  <span id="freeText10188564497526834532" style="display:none">Annie Dillard writes in one of her books about a young student approaching his famous writer/professor. "So you think I can be a great writer?" asks the student. "I don't know. Do you like sentences?"" answers his teacher. I thought about this as I read Amy Hempel's book because I paused after so many sentences while reading. Paused not only for "wow" but paused also for "mmm" and paused also for "ouch", like when the sentence evoked the memory of hurt (to you or by you). If you like to write, this book will help because it shows you as if in slow motion the power of less is more, the eloquence of holding back, the artistry of surprise, of reaching deeper with the unexpected phrase, the quirky twist of words. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10188564497526834532" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4510656307004458591">Contained within these pages are short stories that can make you stop breathing, sentences that I will remember forever and ever, that burned themselves into my brain the second I read them. I was AMAZED to learn that "In the Cemetary where Al Jolson is Buried" was the first piece of fiction she'd EVER written. It's beautiful and real, more real than this keyboard I'm typing on or the trees out my window. I will buy my own copy of this book and re-read these stories for the rest of my life. Each</span>
  <span id="freeText4510656307004458591" style="display:none">Contained within these pages are short stories that can make you stop breathing, sentences that I will remember forever and ever, that burned themselves into my brain the second I read them. I was AMAZED to learn that "In the Cemetary where Al Jolson is Buried" was the first piece of fiction she'd EVER written. It's beautiful and real, more real than this keyboard I'm typing on or the trees out my window. I will buy my own copy of this book and re-read these stories for the rest of my life. Each story is a distillation of a human experience. Her words are precise. Amy Hempel does not waste your time.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer141834334" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1618901418033690095">The Collected Stories of Amy Hempel is actually four books in one. The book pulls together the short stories published in Hempel's collections Reasons to Live (1985), At the Gates of the Animal Kingdom (1990), Tumble Home (1997), and The Dog of the Marriage (2005). Hempel's writing is dark and often funny. Her imagery is always memorable ("her voice has lost weight"). And her observations (via her narrators) about her characters are always telling ("He could not wait to get rid of them so he cou</span>
  <span id="freeText1618901418033690095" style="display:none">The Collected Stories of Amy Hempel is actually four books in one. The book pulls together the short stories published in Hempel's collections Reasons to Live (1985), At the Gates of the Animal Kingdom (1990), Tumble Home (1997), and The Dog of the Marriage (2005). Hempel's writing is dark and often funny. Her imagery is always memorable ("her voice has lost weight"). And her observations (via her narrators) about her characters are always telling ("He could not wait to get rid of them so he could enjoy remembering them"). <br /><br />This isn't a book you read all at once or in a hurry; it's one you savour, story-by-story, when you have time to immerse yourself in the various worlds of Amy Hempel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1618901418033690095" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_4744214" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4744214" />
    <a title="Will" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/289640-will"><img alt="Will" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1219975770p2/289640.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4744214?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will" href="/user/show/289640-will">Will</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4744214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11750817005702072031">It was hard for me to read this book without constantly thinking about the agonizing amount of work that had to go into every single sentence. I kept picturing this frowny-faced, chain-smoking woman slumped over a typewriter desperately trying to warp every word to express exactly what she wanted. The end result was appropriately rewarding. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16130392195016656877">2.5 stars. Good stories keenly observed and literately told. I had a hard time deciding whether to go with 2 or 3 stars. I prefer to read books, whether fiction or nonfiction, that <i>don't</i> seem like the author is either taking him- or herself too seriously or is trying too hard to write capital-L Literature, and Amy Hempel does both. And she's very good at it, which is why, of course, she's beloved of literary critics and writers alike. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_13808764" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13808764" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/422952-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375476320p2/422952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13808764?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/422952-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13808764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8801823271107124788">I have a friend who once wanted to learn to play guitar. But, when he heard Jimi Hendrix play, he decided to give up the instrument because he couldn't imagine himself ever being able to play like that. Amy Hempel makes me feel the same way about writing--my fledgling attempts at writing fiction look clumsy and silly next to Ms. Hempel's elegant and delicate prose.<br/><br/>Ms. Hempel has a reputation for being a minimalist writer. Not being a literature major, I'm unsure of the exact definition of that t</span>
  <span id="freeText8801823271107124788" style="display:none">I have a friend who once wanted to learn to play guitar. But, when he heard Jimi Hendrix play, he decided to give up the instrument because he couldn't imagine himself ever being able to play like that. Amy Hempel makes me feel the same way about writing--my fledgling attempts at writing fiction look clumsy and silly next to Ms. Hempel's elegant and delicate prose.<br /><br />Ms. Hempel has a reputation for being a minimalist writer. Not being a literature major, I'm unsure of the exact definition of that term of art. I'd always thought of minimalism as writing with the short, choppy sentences of Hemingway. Ms. Hemple's version of minimalism is different than that--one more of scope than of style. Her writing reminds me a bit of Chekov. <br /><br />Some of the stories in this collection are funny, some are sad. Most are both sad and funny. If I have any criticism of the book, it's that Ms. Hempel indulges in the moroseness that seems to afflict the vast majority of short story writers. When it comes to short stories, every tickle in the back of one's throat is a fatal condition. Every love affair is a heart-break waiting to happen. And all humor is of the gallows variety (ok, maybe not <i>"all"</i> but a lot). I don't know whether the short story, as a form, encourages despondency or whether despondent people are drawn to write short stories. <br /><br />Either way, reading this book in one sitting might require a prescription for Zoloft. I'm not saying that this is a bad thing, but be prepared for the fact that she's going to get under your skin.<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="8801823271107124788" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/481472966" />
    <a title="Kirby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/281497-kirby"><img alt="Kirby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452468872p2/281497.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/481472966?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirby" href="/user/show/281497-kirby">Kirby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer481472966" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6573973609955091134">Amy Hempel is a pretty pretty cool lady. She writes kind of like Joan Didion, except a little funnier and with more stories about dogs. I really enjoyed this compilation of her four short story books, which is full of simple yet touching musings on California, animals, painters, infidelity, loss -- and starting anew after loss. Many stories touch on dark issues but I would say that overall, her work is quite uplifting. And "The Dog of the Marriage," a story about a woman who trains cute pups to </span>
  <span id="freeText6573973609955091134" style="display:none">Amy Hempel is a pretty pretty cool lady. She writes kind of like Joan Didion, except a little funnier and with more stories about dogs. I really enjoyed this compilation of her four short story books, which is full of simple yet touching musings on California, animals, painters, infidelity, loss -- and starting anew after loss. Many stories touch on dark issues but I would say that overall, her work is quite uplifting. And "The Dog of the Marriage," a story about a woman who trains cute pups to be seeing eye dogs for the blind, was one of the best things I've ever read. Other cool stories include "Pool Night," about diving stunts and losing everything in a fire; "The Children's Party," about a BBQ with a bunch of young parents with young kids at a lake; and a story called "The Most Girl Part of You," which is an incredibly sweet tale of teenage love that touched my heart and made me smile without being overly cheesy or sentimental. <br /><br />Also, one story features a golden retriever named Kirby - and she's a girl! I feel like Amy Hempel and I are meant to be. She writes with a lot of grace and thoughtfulness, and this collection made me a big fan of her work.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6573973609955091134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2262715795046327452" style="display:none">Her sentences are clear and hard and sometimes heavy, like glass I-beams. That's a bad metaphor, because they aren't transparent; the sentences are the things you notice the most. <br /><br />I first read Amy Hempel's story, "The Harvest," a while ago. I don't remember who told me about it. Someone in a writing group somewhere. I found it online and thought it was the most compressed, sharp blade of a story I'd ever read. I saved it somewhere on a now-defunct laptop. But now I've got the collection. <br /><br />Most of the stories are like "The Harvest": short, straight, with beautifully focused writing. And I mean short. Some are two pages--but a couple are just one sentence! The longest, "Tumble Home," is well worth stretching your attention span for. All the stories leave you with something: a phrase, an image, an idea, an emotion, a thirst. <br /><br />Favorite story: The Dog of the Marriage. <br />Favorite quote: can't find it now, but it goes like this: (in a dog collar store in Paris) "'What kind of dog is he?'         <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12410698?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eileen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eileen" href="/user/show/280241-eileen">Eileen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12410698" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5622656812456871020">This is a book that many fiction writers have been waiting for for a long time, especially since Hempel's "Reasons to Live" has been out of print for a while. Essentially, this omnibus collection gathers together Hempel's four books of short fiction, including her most recent "The Dog of the Marriage." The book has been widely lauded in the press, but I will just say here that if you are looking to read some short stories by a master of minimalist fiction whose work is wryly funny and sad at the</span>
  <span id="freeText5622656812456871020" style="display:none">This is a book that many fiction writers have been waiting for for a long time, especially since Hempel's "Reasons to Live" has been out of print for a while. Essentially, this omnibus collection gathers together Hempel's four books of short fiction, including her most recent "The Dog of the Marriage." The book has been widely lauded in the press, but I will just say here that if you are looking to read some short stories by a master of minimalist fiction whose work is wryly funny and sad at the same time, this is it. Some pieces are experimentalist, some more straightforward in their "realism," but all of them are beautifully written, and yes, there are many dogs and cats who make appearances. Hempel recently embarked on a new career in forensic pathology [seriously!] and these stories show how well-suited she would be for a profession that pays close attention to the minute details of both normal and abnormal life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5622656812456871020" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154820372" />
    <a title="Julie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3960478-julie"><img alt="Julie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1382364061p2/3960478.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/154820372?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie" href="/user/show/3960478-julie">Julie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743291637" href="/book/show/841644.The_Collected_Stories_of_Amy_Hempel">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who love short stories, writers</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3960478-julie?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3960478-julie?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3960478-julie?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer154820372" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6448691831259486723">Hempel is amazing. Her prose is as crisp and sharp as you'll find and yet, enviably, appears effortless. Her style reminds me of a writing instructor of mine, who would always ask us: "Is every word in that sentence necessary? How much would you pay for that one there?" The lesson being, if you wouldn't lay your money on the line for any extraneous words, why include them? If Hempel took my instructor's class, she would be the teacher's pet. Every word in Hempel's stories has a purpose and every</span>
  <span id="freeText6448691831259486723" style="display:none">Hempel is amazing. Her prose is as crisp and sharp as you'll find and yet, enviably, appears effortless. Her style reminds me of a writing instructor of mine, who would always ask us: "Is every word in that sentence necessary? How much would you pay for that one there?" The lesson being, if you wouldn't lay your money on the line for any extraneous words, why include them? If Hempel took my instructor's class, she would be the teacher's pet. Every word in Hempel's stories has a purpose and every word has a place. The result is stunning. She's definitely a "writer's writer," if there is such a thing. She challenges me to be better with every sentence. <br /><br />The majority of Hempel's stories are only a few pages long. Their brevity makes them perfect to read on my morning bus commute. They're a wonderful start and end to my day - witty, observant, and full of pathos.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6448691831259486723" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_69215885" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743291637" href="/book/show/841644.The_Collected_Stories_of_Amy_Hempel">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Nobody</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69215885" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15888453460887077732">I <i>tried</i> to like her stories, but after making it about a third of the way through this book, I gave up -- something I rarely do. The writing just seemed too affected, too deliberately offhand. Try as I might, I simply could not connect with her characters, and I simply could not relate to the universe they inhabited. A quick example: In one story, a character is said to hurl his wife's false teeth "like a discus." Sorry, that is simply not believable, and seems to have been written by someone wh</span>
  <span id="freeText15888453460887077732" style="display:none">I <i>tried</i> to like her stories, but after making it about a third of the way through this book, I gave up -- something I rarely do. The writing just seemed too affected, too deliberately offhand. Try as I might, I simply could not connect with her characters, and I simply could not relate to the universe they inhabited. A quick example: In one story, a character is said to hurl his wife's false teeth "like a discus." Sorry, that is simply not believable, and seems to have been written by someone who has no idea what it is to hurl either teeth or a discus. I really enjoy Raymond Carver, so it is not that I am opposed to "minimalism," but I just could not get through this book. YMMV. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hUQ96Lewdxrsya/UsWYhAKbOoLtq8uip3M/D/Th7/aCn+jBPU052pDzdLgI8oIhrgte3cfsvZu5jtqnVgAe+1w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

As they confront an array of common catastrophes - a colicky baby, a faltering marriage, stalled ambitions - the wife analyzes her predicament, invoking everything from Keats and Kafka to the thought experiments of the Stoics to the lessons of doomed Russian cosmonauts. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50346183-gotico-americano"><img alt="Gotico americano" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1578386510l/50346183._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_50346183'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50346183-gotico-americano?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Gotico americano<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/19899534.Arianna_Farinelli?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Arianna Farinelli<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.82 avg rating &mdash; 204 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2020\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer4374451150218562911\">È la notte delle elezioni. Bruna – che insegna Scienze politiche in un college di New York – è stata in tv per commentarne i risultati, ma l’angoscia che prova rientrando a casa non è dovuta alla vittoria del candidato repubblicano bensì al segreto c<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4374451150218562911\" style=\"display:none\">È la notte delle elezioni. Bruna – che insegna Scienze politiche in un college di New York – è stata in tv per commentarne i risultati, ma l’angoscia che prova rientrando a casa non è dovuta alla vittoria del candidato repubblicano bensì al segreto che sa di dover confessare a suo marito Tom. Da tempo intorno al loro matrimonio si affollano lunghe ombre: quella dei genitori di Tom, italoamericani perbenisti, radicalmente conservatori come tanti immigrati delle generazioni venute dopo la prima; l’ombra del tormento del figlio Mario, che manifesta un precoce disagio verso il suo corpo maschile; quella dell’alien number attribuito a Bruna dalla burocrazia statunitense.\nQuesto libro è la storia di una famiglia, dei suoi segreti, delle sfide a cui è chiamata, ma è anche un appello rivolto a tutti noi. Yunus, il giovane studente afroamericano con il quale Bruna ha intrecciato una relazione, le lascia infatti un memoriale che è al tempo stesso una requisitoria contro l’ipocrisia delle nostre democrazie occidentali, un romanzo nel romanzo – la storia di un ragazzo per il quale l’estremismo religioso è la sola via per sentirsi fedele a qualcosa di grande – e una lettera d’amore.\nCiascuno dei protagonisti cammina solo, dentro un buio più forte delle luci di Manhattan, alla ostinata ricerca della propria identità. Arianna Farinelli fa della diversità – etnica, culturale, religiosa, di genere – la lente attraverso cui misurare il mondo in cui viviamo. Ci accoglie tra le ovattate moquette dell’élite occidentale, poi spalanca sotto i nostri piedi la voragine delle ipocrisie che la mettono in pericolo. E attraverso la voce di Yunus ci addita come specchio il quadro di Grant Wood, American Gothic: “Facce bianche di vecchi impauriti che pensano di proteggere il mondo con un forcone, ma il loro mondo già non esiste più”. È la voce di chi ha perduto tutto, tranne la speranza che le parole possano costruire ponti verso un futuro di uguaglianza e libertà.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4374451150218562911\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer wtrSignedOut\' id=\'25_book_50346183\'>\n<div class=\'wtrUp wtrLeft\'>\n<form <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15029277197077440211">Amy Hempel is an American short story writer, journalist, and university professor at Brooklyn College. Hempel was a former student of Gordon Lish, who eventually helped her publish her first collection of short stories. Hempel has been published in Harper's, Vanity Fair, GQ, and Bomb. She is the recipient of a Guggenheim Fellowship, as well as the Ambassador Book Award in 2007, the Rea Award for </span>
  <span id="freeText15029277197077440211" style="display:none">Amy Hempel is an American short story writer, journalist, and university professor at Brooklyn College. Hempel was a former student of Gordon Lish, who eventually helped her publish her first collection of short stories. Hempel has been published in Harper's, Vanity Fair, GQ, and Bomb. She is the recipient of a Guggenheim Fellowship, as well as the Ambassador Book Award in 2007, the Rea Award for the Short Story in 2008, and the Pen/Malamud Award for short fiction in 2009.</span>
